</div><div class="mw-content-ltr mw-parser-output" lang="en" dir="ltr"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Birds from the order Strigiformes</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1320445320">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}@media print{body.ns-0 .mw-parser-output .hatnote{display:none!important}}</style><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Owl_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Owl (disambiguation)">Owl (disambiguation)</a>.</div>

<tr>

</th></tr>
<tr>
<td colspan="2" style="text-align: left">
<p>Strigidae <small><i>sensu</i> Sibley &amp; Ahlquist</small>
</p>
</td></tr>

</tbody></table><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1289143401">@media(max-width:640px){body:not(.skin-minerva) .mw-parser-output .infobox{width:100%!important}body:not(.skin-minerva) .mw-parser-output .infobox th{width:50%}}@media screen{html.skin-theme-clientpref-night .mw-parser-output .infobox.biota tr{background:transparent!important}html.skin-theme-clientpref-night .mw-parser-output .infobox.biota img{background:transparent}}@media screen and (prefers-color-scheme:dark){html.skin-theme-clientpref-os .mw-parser-output .infobox.biota tr{background:transparent!important}html.skin-theme-clientpref-os .mw-parser-output .infobox.biota img{background:white}}.mw-parser-output .infobox.biota .taxobox-edit-taxonomy img{background:transparent!important}body.skin-vector .mw-parser-output table.biota.infobox{margin-top:0.5em}body.skin--responsive .mw-parser-output table.biota.infobox tr.taxonrow2 td{padding:2px 10px}</style>
<p><b>Owls</b> are <a href="/wiki/Bird" title="Bird">birds</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Order_(biology)" title="Order (biology)">order</a> <b>Strigiformes</b><sup id="cite&#95;ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="&#39;s&#39; in &#39;sigh&#39;">s</span><span title="&#39;t&#39; in &#39;tie&#39;">t</span><span title="&#39;r&#39; in &#39;rye&#39;">r</span><span title="/ɪ/: &#39;i&#39; in &#39;kit&#39;">ɪ</span><span title="/dʒ/: &#39;j&#39; in &#39;jam&#39;">dʒ</span><span title="/ə/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;about&#39;">ə</span><span title="&#39;f&#39; in &#39;find&#39;">f</span><span title="/ɔːr/: &#39;ar&#39; in &#39;war&#39;">ɔːr</span><span title="&#39;m&#39; in &#39;my&#39;">m</span><span title="/iː/: &#39;ee&#39; in &#39;fleece&#39;">iː</span><span title="&#39;z&#39; in &#39;zoom&#39;">z</span></span>/</a></span></span>), which includes over 200 species of mostly <a href="/wiki/Solitary_animal" class="mw-redirect" title="Solitary animal">solitary</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nocturnal_animal" title="Nocturnal animal">nocturnal</a> <a href="/wiki/Birds_of_prey" class="mw-redirect" title="Birds of prey">birds of prey</a> typified by an upright stance, a large, broad head, <a href="/wiki/Binocular_vision" title="Binocular vision">binocular vision</a>, <a href="/wiki/Binaural_hearing" class="mw-redirect" title="Binaural hearing">binaural hearing</a>, sharp <a href="/wiki/Claw" title="Claw">talons</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Feathers" class="mw-redirect" title="Feathers">feathers</a> adapted for silent flight. Exceptions include the diurnal <a href="/wiki/Northern_hawk-owl" title="Northern hawk-owl">northern hawk-owl</a> and the gregarious <a href="/wiki/Burrowing_owl" title="Burrowing owl">burrowing owl</a>.
</p><p>Owls are divided into two <a href="/wiki/Family_(biology)" title="Family (biology)">families</a>: the true (or typical) owl family, <a href="/wiki/Strigidae" title="Strigidae">Strigidae</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Barn_owl" title="Barn owl">barn owl</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bay_owl" title="Bay owl">bay owl</a> family, <a href="/wiki/Tytonidae" title="Tytonidae">Tytonidae</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-ioc&#95;2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ioc-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Owls hunt mostly small <a href="/wiki/Mammal" title="Mammal">mammals</a>, <a href="/wiki/Insect" title="Insect">insects</a>, and other birds, although a few species specialize in hunting <a href="/wiki/Fish" title="Fish">fish</a>. They are found in all regions of the Earth except the polar ice caps and some remote islands.
</p><p>A group of owls is called a "parliament".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" />
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Anatomy">Anatomy</h2></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-left" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Chouettes.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Chouettes.jpg/250px-Chouettes.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="167" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Chouettes.jpg/500px-Chouettes.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2816" data-file-height="1880" /></a><figcaption><a href="/wiki/Tawny_owl" title="Tawny owl">Tawny owl</a> (<i>Strix aluco</i>), left, and <a href="/wiki/Western_barn_owl" title="Western barn owl">western barn owl</a> (<i>Tyto alba</i>), right, belonging respectively to the <a href="/wiki/Strigidae" title="Strigidae">Strigidae</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tytonidae" title="Tytonidae">Tytonidae</a> families</figcaption></figure>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Athene_cuniculariaa.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Athene_cuniculariaa.jpg/250px-Athene_cuniculariaa.jpg" decoding="async" width="190" height="242" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Athene_cuniculariaa.jpg/500px-Athene_cuniculariaa.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1216" data-file-height="1549" /></a><figcaption><a href="/wiki/Burrowing_owl" title="Burrowing owl">Burrowing owl</a> (<i>Athene cunicularia</i>)</figcaption></figure>
<p>Owls possess large, forward-facing eyes and ear-holes, a <a href="/wiki/Hawk" title="Hawk">hawk</a>-like <a href="/wiki/Beak" title="Beak">beak</a>, a flat face, and usually a conspicuous circle of feathers, a <a href="/wiki/Facial_disc" title="Facial disc">facial disc</a>, around each eye. The feathers making up this disc can be adjusted to sharply focus sounds from varying distances onto the owls' asymmetrically placed ear cavities. Most birds of prey have eyes on the sides of their heads, but the stereoscopic nature of the owl's forward-facing eyes permits the greater sense of <a href="/wiki/Depth_perception" title="Depth perception">depth perception</a> necessary for low-light hunting. Owls have <a href="/wiki/Binocular_vision" title="Binocular vision">binocular vision</a>, but they must rotate their entire heads to change the focus of their view because, like most birds, their eyes are fixed in their sockets. Owls are farsighted and cannot clearly see anything nearer than a few centimetres of their eyes. Caught prey can be felt by owls with the use of <a href="/wiki/Filoplume" class="mw-redirect" title="Filoplume">filoplumes</a>—hairlike feathers on the beak and feet that act as "feelers". Their far vision, particularly in low light, is exceptionally good.
</p><p>Owls can rotate their heads and necks as much as 270°. Owls have 14 neck vertebrae—humans have only seven—and their vertebral circulatory systems are adapted to allow them to rotate their heads without <a href="/wiki/Cerebral_infarction" title="Cerebral infarction">cutting off blood to the brain</a>. Specifically, the <a href="/wiki/Intervertebral_foramina" class="mw-redirect" title="Intervertebral foramina">foramina</a> in their vertebrae through which the vertebral arteries pass are about ten times the diameter of the artery, instead of about the same size as the artery, as is the case in humans; the vertebral arteries enter the <a href="/wiki/Cervical_vertebra" class="mw-redirect" title="Cervical vertebra">cervical vertebrae</a> higher than in other birds, giving the vessels some slack, and the <a href="/wiki/Carotid_artery" title="Carotid artery">carotid arteries</a> unite in a very large <a href="/wiki/Anastomosis" title="Anastomosis">anastomosis</a> or junction, the largest of any bird's, preventing blood supply from being cut off while they rotate their necks. Other anastomoses between the carotid and vertebral arteries support this effect.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The smallest owl—weighing as little as <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1154941027">.mw-parser-output .frac{white-space:nowrap}.mw-parser-output .frac .num,.mw-parser-output .frac .den{font-size:80%;line-height:0;vertical-align:super}.mw-parser-output .frac .den{vertical-align:sub}.mw-parser-output .sr-only{border:0;clip:rect(0,0,0,0);clip-path:polygon(0px 0px,0px 0px,0px 0px);height:1px;margin:-1px;overflow:hidden;padding:0;position:absolute;width:1px}</style>31&#160;g (<span class="frac">1<span class="sr-only">+</span><span class="num">3</span>&#8260;<span class="den">32</span></span>&#160;oz) and measuring some <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1154941027" />13.5&#160;cm (<span class="frac">5<span class="sr-only">+</span><span class="num">1</span>&#8260;<span class="den">4</span></span>&#160;in)—is the <a href="/wiki/Elf_owl" title="Elf owl">elf owl</a> (<i>Micrathene whitneyi</i>).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Owls&#95;6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Owls-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>  Around the same diminutive length, although slightly heavier, are the lesser known <a href="/wiki/Long-whiskered_owlet" title="Long-whiskered owlet">long-whiskered owlet</a> (<i>Xenoglaux loweryi</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Tamaulipas_pygmy_owl" title="Tamaulipas pygmy owl">Tamaulipas pygmy owl</a> (<i>Glaucidium sanchezi</i>).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Owls&#95;6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Owls-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The largest owls are two similarly sized species; the <a href="/wiki/Eurasian_eagle-owl" title="Eurasian eagle-owl">Eurasian eagle-owl</a> (<i>Bubo bubo</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Blakiston%27s_fish_owl" title="Blakiston&#39;s fish owl">Blakiston's fish owl</a> (<i>Ketupa blakistoni</i>). The largest females of these species are 71&#160;cm (28&#160;in) long, have a 190&#160;cm (75&#160;in) wing span, and weigh <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1154941027" />4.2&#160;kg (<span class="frac">9<span class="sr-only">+</span><span class="num">1</span>&#8260;<span class="den">4</span></span>&#160;lb).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Owls&#95;6-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Owls-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>7<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>10<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Different species of owls produce different sounds; this distribution of calls aids owls in finding mates or announcing their presence to potential competitors, and also aids <a href="/wiki/Ornithologist" class="mw-redirect" title="Ornithologist">ornithologists</a> and <a href="/wiki/Birder" class="mw-redirect" title="Birder">birders</a> in locating these birds and distinguishing species. As noted above, their facial discs help owls to funnel the sound of prey to their ears. In many species, these discs are placed asymmetrically, for better directional location.
</p><p>Owl <a href="/wiki/Plumage" title="Plumage">plumage</a> is generally <a href="/wiki/Crypsis" title="Crypsis">cryptic</a>, although several species have facial and head markings, including face masks, <a href="/wiki/Ear_tuft" title="Ear tuft">ear tufts</a>, and brightly colored <a href="/wiki/Iris_(anatomy)" title="Iris (anatomy)">irises</a>. These markings are generally more common in species inhabiting open habitats, and are thought to be used in signaling with other owls in low-light conditions.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>11<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Sexual_dimorphism">Sexual dimorphism</h3></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Owl._Birds_-_defenders_of_the_forest._USSR_stamp._1979.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Owl._Birds_-_defenders_of_the_forest._USSR_stamp._1979.jpg/250px-Owl._Birds_-_defenders_of_the_forest._USSR_stamp._1979.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="179" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Owl._Birds_-_defenders_of_the_forest._USSR_stamp._1979.jpg/500px-Owl._Birds_-_defenders_of_the_forest._USSR_stamp._1979.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2479" data-file-height="1774" /></a><figcaption>A stamp from the Soviet Union, 1979</figcaption></figure>
<p><a href="/wiki/Sexual_dimorphism" title="Sexual dimorphism">Sexual dimorphism</a> is a physical difference between males and females of a species. Female owls are typically larger than the males.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:0&#95;12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The degree of size dimorphism varies across multiple populations and species, and is measured through various traits, such as wing span and body mass.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:0&#95;12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>One theory suggests that selection has led males to be smaller because it allows them to be efficient <a href="/wiki/Foraging" title="Foraging">foragers</a>. The ability to obtain more food is advantageous during breeding season. In some species, female owls stay at their nest with their eggs while it is the responsibility of the male to bring back food to the nest.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:2&#95;13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-13"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> If food is scarce, the male first feeds himself before feeding the female.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:1&#95;14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Small birds, which are agile, are an important source of food for owls. Male burrowing owls have been observed to have longer wing chords than females, despite being smaller than females.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:1&#95;14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Furthermore, owls have been observed to be roughly the same size as their prey.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:1&#95;14-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This has also been observed in other predatory birds,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:2&#95;13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-13"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> which suggests that owls with smaller bodies and long wing chords have been selected for because of the increased agility and speed that allows them to catch their prey.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>Another popular theory suggests that females have not been selected to be smaller like male owls because of their sexual roles. In many species, female owls may not leave the nest. Therefore, females may have a larger mass to allow them to go for a longer period of time without starving. For example, one hypothesized sexual role is that larger females are more capable of dismembering prey and feeding it to their young, hence female owls are larger than their male counterparts.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:0&#95;12-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>A different theory suggests that the size difference between male and females is due to sexual selection: since large females can choose their mate and may violently reject a male's sexual advances, smaller male owls that have the ability to escape unreceptive females are more likely to have been selected.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:1&#95;14-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>If the character is stable, there can be different optimums for both sexes. Selection operates on both sexes at the same time; therefore it is necessary to explain not only why one of the sexes is relatively bigger, but also why the other sex is smaller.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> If owls are still evolving toward smaller bodies and longer wing chords, according to V. Geodakyan's Evolutionary Theory of Sex, males should be more advanced on these characters. Males are viewed as an evolutionary vanguard of a population, and sexual dimorphism on the character, as an evolutionary "distance" between the sexes.  "Phylogenetic rule of sexual dimorphism" states that if there exists a sexual dimorphism on any character, then the evolution of this trait goes from the female form toward the male one.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Hunting_adaptations">Hunting adaptations</h3></div>
<p>All owls are carnivorous birds of prey and live on diets of insects, small rodents and lagomorphs. Some owls are also specifically adapted to hunt fish. They are very adept in hunting in their respective environments. Since owls can be found in nearly all parts of the world and across a multitude of ecosystems, their hunting skills and characteristics vary slightly from species to species, though most characteristics are shared among all species.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>17<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Flight_and_feathers">Flight and feathers</h4></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1316064257">.mw-parser-output .infobox-subbox{padding:0;border:none;margin:-3px;width:auto;min-width:100%;font-size:100%;clear:none;float:none;background-color:transparent;color:inherit}.mw-parser-output .infobox-3cols-child{margin:-3px}.mw-parser-output .infobox .navbar{font-size:100%}@media screen{html.skin-theme-clientpref-night .mw-parser-output .infobox-full-data:not(.notheme)>div:not(.notheme)[style]{background:#1f1f23!important;color:#f8f9fa}}@media screen and (prefers-color-scheme:dark){html.skin-theme-clientpref-os .mw-parser-output .infobox-full-data:not(.notheme)>div:not(.notheme)[style]{background:#1f1f23!important;color:#f8f9fa}}@media(min-width:640px){body.skin--responsive .mw-parser-output .infobox-table{display:table!important}body.skin--responsive .mw-parser-output .infobox-table>caption{display:table-caption!important}body.skin--responsive .mw-parser-output .infobox-table>tbody{display:table-row-group}body.skin--responsive .mw-parser-output .infobox-table th,body.skin--responsive .mw-parser-output .infobox-table td{padding-left:inherit;padding-right:inherit}}</style><table class="infobox" style="width: 230px; clear: right; float:right;margin:0 0 4px 1.5em"><tbody><tr><th colspan="2" class="infobox-above" style="font-size:115%">External videos</th></tr><tr><td colspan="2" class="infobox-full-data" style="text-align: left"><span class="noviewer" typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="video icon" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg/20px-Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg.png" decoding="async" width="16" height="16" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg/40px-Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="128" data-file-height="128" /></a></span> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=d_FEaFgJyfA">Experiment! How Does An Owl Fly So Silently?</a>, from <a href="/wiki/BBC_Earth" title="BBC Earth">BBC Earth</a></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Most owls share an innate ability to fly almost silently and also more slowly in comparison to other birds of prey. Most owls live a mainly nocturnal lifestyle and being able to fly without making any noise gives them a strong advantage over prey alert to the slightest sound in the night. A silent, slow flight is not as necessary for diurnal and crepuscular owls given that prey can usually see an owl approaching. Owls' feathers are generally larger than the average birds' feathers, have fewer radiates, longer pennulum, and achieve smooth edges with different <a href="/wiki/Rachis" title="Rachis">rachis</a> structures.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Bachmann2007&#95;18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bachmann2007-18"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>18<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Serrated edges along the owl's <a href="/wiki/Flight_feather#Remiges" title="Flight feather">remiges</a> bring the flapping of the wing down to a nearly silent mechanism. The serrations are more likely reducing aerodynamic disturbances, rather than simply reducing noise.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The surface of the flight feathers is covered with a velvety structure that absorbs the sound of the wing moving. These unique structures reduce noise frequencies above 2&#160;kHz,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Neuhaus1973&#95;20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Neuhaus1973-20"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>20<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> making the sound level emitted drop below the typical hearing spectrum of the owl's usual prey<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Neuhaus1973&#95;20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Neuhaus1973-20"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>20<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Willott&#95;21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Willott-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and also within the owl's own best hearing range.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>22<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>23<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This optimizes the owl's ability to silently fly to capture prey without the prey hearing the owl first as it flies, and to hear any noise the prey makes. It also allows the owl to monitor the sound output from its flight pattern.
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Great_Horned_Owl_in_a_Rain_Storm_in_the_Mojave.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Great_Horned_Owl_in_a_Rain_Storm_in_the_Mojave.jpg/250px-Great_Horned_Owl_in_a_Rain_Storm_in_the_Mojave.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="303" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Great_Horned_Owl_in_a_Rain_Storm_in_the_Mojave.jpg/500px-Great_Horned_Owl_in_a_Rain_Storm_in_the_Mojave.jpg 2x" data-file-width="989" data-file-height="1200" /></a><figcaption>A <a href="/wiki/Great_horned_owl" title="Great horned owl">great horned owl</a> with wet <a href="/wiki/Feathers" class="mw-redirect" title="Feathers">feathers</a>, waiting out a <a href="/wiki/Rainstorm" class="mw-redirect" title="Rainstorm">rainstorm</a></figcaption></figure>
<p>The disadvantage of such feather adaptations for barn owls is that their feathers are not waterproof.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-auto&#95;24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-24"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>24<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The adaptations mean that barn owls do not use the <a href="/wiki/Uropygial_gland" title="Uropygial gland">uropygial gland</a>, informally the "preen" or "oil" gland, as most birds do, to spread oils across their plumage through preening.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>25<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This makes them highly vulnerable to heavy rain when they are unable to hunt.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>26<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Historically, they would switch to hunting indoors in wet weather, using <a href="/wiki/Barn" title="Barn">barns</a> and other agricultural buildings, but the decline in the numbers of these structures in the 20th and 21st centuries has reduced such opportunities.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-auto&#95;24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-auto-24"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>24<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The lack of waterproofing means that barn owls are also susceptible to drowning, in <a href="/wiki/Manger" title="Manger">drinking troughs</a> and other structures with smooth sides. The <a href="/wiki/Barn_Owl_Trust" title="Barn Owl Trust">Barn Owl Trust</a> provides advice on how this can be mitigated, by the installation of floats.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>27<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Vision">Vision</h4></div>
<p>Eyesight is a particular characteristic of the owl that aids in nocturnal prey capture. Owls are part of a small group of birds that live nocturnally, but do not use <a href="/wiki/Animal_echolocation" title="Animal echolocation">echolocation</a> to guide them in flight in low-light situations. Owls are known for their disproportionally large eyes in comparison to their skulls. An apparent consequence of the evolution of an absolutely large eye in a relatively small skull is that the eye of the owl has become tubular in shape. This shape is found in other so-called nocturnal eyes, such as the eyes of <a href="/wiki/Strepsirrhini" title="Strepsirrhini">strepsirrhine</a> <a href="/wiki/Primate" title="Primate">primates</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bathypelagic_fish" class="mw-redirect" title="Bathypelagic fish">bathypelagic fishes</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Walls1942&#95;28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Walls1942-28"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>28<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Since the eyes are fixed into these sclerotic tubes, they are unable to move the eyes in any direction.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-König1999&#95;29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-König1999-29"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>29<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Instead of moving their eyes, owls swivel their heads to view their surroundings. Owls' heads are capable of swiveling through an angle of roughly 270° in either direction, easily enabling them to see behind them without relocating the torso.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-König1999&#95;29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-König1999-29"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>29<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This ability keeps bodily movement at a minimum, thus reduces the amount of sound the owl makes as it waits for its prey. Owls are regarded as having the most frontally placed eyes among all avian groups, which gives them some of the largest binocular fields of vision. Owls are farsighted and cannot focus on objects within a few centimetres of their eyes.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Walls1942&#95;28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Walls1942-28"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>28<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Hughes&#95;30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hughes-30"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>30<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> These mechanisms are only able to function due to the large-sized retinal image.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Martin1982&#95;31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Martin1982-31"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>31<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Thus, the primary nocturnal function in the vision of the owl is due to its large posterior nodal distance; retinal image brightness is only maximized to the owl within secondary neural functions.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Martin1982&#95;31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Martin1982-31"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>31<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> These attributes of the owl cause its nocturnal eyesight to be far superior to that of its average prey.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Martin1982&#95;31-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Martin1982-31"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>31<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Hearing">Hearing</h4></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Great_Horned_Owl_at_twilight,_Mojave_Desert.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Great_Horned_Owl_at_twilight%2C_Mojave_Desert.jpg/250px-Great_Horned_Owl_at_twilight%2C_Mojave_Desert.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="178" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Great_Horned_Owl_at_twilight%2C_Mojave_Desert.jpg/500px-Great_Horned_Owl_at_twilight%2C_Mojave_Desert.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1599" data-file-height="1141" /></a><figcaption>A <a href="/wiki/Great_horned_owl" title="Great horned owl">great horned owl</a> perched on the top of a Joshua tree at evening (<a href="/wiki/Twilight" title="Twilight">twilight</a>) in the <a href="/wiki/Mojave_Desert" title="Mojave Desert">Mojave Desert</a>, U.S.</figcaption></figure>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Sound_localization_in_owls" title="Sound localization in owls">Sound localization in owls</a></div>
<p>Owls exhibit specialized hearing functions and ear shapes that also aid in hunting. They are noted for asymmetrical ear placements on the skull in some genera. Owls can have either internal or external ears, both of which are asymmetrical. Asymmetry has not been reported to extend to the middle or internal ear of the owl. Asymmetrical ear placement on the skull allows the owl to pinpoint the location of its prey. This is especially true for strictly nocturnal species such as the barn owls <i>Tyto</i> or Tengmalm's owl.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-König1999&#95;29-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-König1999-29"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>29<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> With ears set at different places on its skull, an owl is able to determine the direction from which the sound is coming by the minute difference in time that it takes for the sound waves to penetrate the left and right ears.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The owl turns its head until the sound reaches both ears at the same time, at which point it is directly facing the source of the sound. This time difference between ears is about 30 microseconds.  Behind the ear openings are modified, dense feathers, densely packed to form a facial ruff, which creates an anterior-facing, concave wall that cups the sound into the ear structure.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Norberg&#95;33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Norberg-33"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>33<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This facial ruff is poorly defined in some species, and prominent, nearly encircling the face, in other species. The facial disk also acts to direct sound into the ears, and a downward-facing, sharply triangular beak minimizes sound reflection away from the face. The shape of the facial disk is adjustable at will to focus sounds more effectively.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-König1999&#95;29-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-König1999-29"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>29<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The prominences above a great horned owl's head are commonly mistaken as its ears. This is not the case; they are merely feather tufts. The ears are on the sides of the head in the usual location (in two different locations as described above).
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Talons">Talons</h4></div>
<p>While the auditory and visual capabilities of the owl allow it to locate and pursue its prey, the talons and beak of the owl do the final work. The owl kills its prey using these talons to crush the skull and knead the body.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-König1999&#95;29-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-König1999-29"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>29<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The crushing power of an owl's talons varies according to prey size and type, and by the size of the owl. The burrowing owl (<i>Athene cunicularia</i>), a small, partly insectivorous owl, has a release force of only 5&#160;N. The larger <a href="/wiki/Western_barn_owl" title="Western barn owl">barn owl</a> (<i>Tyto alba</i>) needs a force of 30&#160;N to release its prey, and one of the largest owls, the <a href="/wiki/Great_horned_owl" title="Great horned owl">great horned owl</a> (<i>Bubo virginianus</i>), needs a force over 130&#160;N to release prey in its talons.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Marti1974&#95;34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marti1974-34"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>34<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> An owl's talons, like those of most birds of prey, can seem massive in comparison to the body size outside of flight. The <a href="/wiki/Tyto_novaehollandiae_castanops" class="mw-redirect" title="Tyto novaehollandiae castanops">Tasmanian masked owl</a> has some of the proportionally longest talons of any bird of prey; they appear enormous in comparison to the body when fully extended to grasp prey.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Einoder2007&#95;35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Einoder2007-35"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>35<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> An owl's claws are sharp and curved. The family Tytonidae has inner and central toes of about equal length, while the family Strigidae has an inner toe that is distinctly shorter than the central one.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Marti1974&#95;34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marti1974-34"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>34<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> These different morphologies allow efficiency in capturing prey specific to the different environments they inhabit.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Beak">Beak</h4></div>
<p>The beak of the owl is short, curved, and downward-facing, and typically hooked at the tip for gripping and tearing its prey. Once prey is captured, the scissor motion of the top and lower bill is used to tear the tissue and kill. The sharp lower edge of the upper bill works in coordination with the sharp upper edge of the lower bill to deliver this motion. The downward-facing beak allows the owl's field of vision to be clear, as well as directing sound into the ears without deflecting sound waves away from the face.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>36<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Camouflage">Camouflage</h4></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Harfang_en_vol_1.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Harfang_en_vol_1.jpg/250px-Harfang_en_vol_1.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="169" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Harfang_en_vol_1.jpg/500px-Harfang_en_vol_1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="811" /></a><figcaption>The <a href="/wiki/Snowy_owl" title="Snowy owl">snowy owl</a> has effective <a href="/wiki/Snow_camouflage" title="Snow camouflage">snow camouflage</a></figcaption></figure>
<p>The coloration of the owl's plumage plays a key role in its ability to sit still and blend into the environment, making it nearly invisible to prey. Owls tend to mimic the coloration and sometimes the texture patterns of their surroundings, the barn owl being an exception. The <a href="/wiki/Snowy_owl" title="Snowy owl">snowy owl</a> (<i>Bubo scandiacus</i>) appears nearly bleach-white in color with a few flecks of black, mimicking their snowy surroundings perfectly, while the speckled brown plumage of the <a href="/wiki/Tawny_owl" title="Tawny owl">tawny owl</a> (<i>Strix aluco</i>) allows it to lie in wait among the deciduous woodland it prefers for its habitat. Likewise, the <a href="/wiki/Mottled_wood_owl" title="Mottled wood owl">mottled wood owl</a> (<i>Strix ocellata</i>) displays shades of brown, tan, and black, making the owl nearly invisible in the surrounding trees, especially from behind. Usually, the only tell-tale sign of a perched owl is its vocalizations or its vividly colored eyes.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Behavior">Behavior</h2></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Feathers_owl_vs_hawk.png" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Feathers_owl_vs_hawk.png/250px-Feathers_owl_vs_hawk.png" decoding="async" width="250" height="243" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Feathers_owl_vs_hawk.png/500px-Feathers_owl_vs_hawk.png 2x" data-file-width="1312" data-file-height="1277" /></a><figcaption>Comparison of an owl (left) and hawk (right) <a href="/wiki/Remex" class="mw-redirect" title="Remex">remex</a>.</figcaption></figure>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:EulenfederTeil3.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/EulenfederTeil3.jpg/250px-EulenfederTeil3.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="188" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/EulenfederTeil3.jpg/500px-EulenfederTeil3.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2272" data-file-height="1704" /></a><figcaption>The <a href="/wiki/Serration" title="Serration">serrations</a> on the leading edge of an owl's flight <a href="/wiki/Feather" title="Feather">feathers</a> reduce noise</figcaption></figure>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:SpottedEagleOwl2539MGEyeLid.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/SpottedEagleOwl2539MGEyeLid.jpg/250px-SpottedEagleOwl2539MGEyeLid.jpg" decoding="async" width="190" height="253" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/SpottedEagleOwl2539MGEyeLid.jpg/500px-SpottedEagleOwl2539MGEyeLid.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2288" data-file-height="3051" /></a><figcaption>Owl eyes each have <a href="/wiki/Nictitating_membrane" title="Nictitating membrane">nictitating membranes</a> that can move independently of each other, as seen on this <a href="/wiki/Spotted_eagle-owl" title="Spotted eagle-owl">spotted eagle-owl</a> in <a href="/wiki/Johannesburg" title="Johannesburg">Johannesburg</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a>.</figcaption></figure> 
<p>Most owls are <a href="/wiki/Nocturnal" class="mw-redirect" title="Nocturnal">nocturnal</a>, actively hunting their prey in darkness. Several types of owls are <a href="/wiki/Crepuscular" class="mw-redirect" title="Crepuscular">crepuscular</a>—active during the twilight hours of dawn and dusk; for example the <a href="/wiki/Pygmy_owl" title="Pygmy owl">pygmy owls</a> (<i>Glaucidium</i>). A few owls are active during the day, also; examples are the <a href="/wiki/Burrowing_owl" title="Burrowing owl">burrowing owl</a> (<i>Speotyto cunicularia</i>) and the <a href="/wiki/Short-eared_owl" title="Short-eared owl">short-eared owl</a> (<i>Asio flammeus</i>).
</p><p>Much of the owls' hunting strategy depends on stealth and surprise. Owls have at least two adaptations that aid them in achieving stealth. First, the dull coloration of their feathers can render them almost invisible under certain conditions. Secondly, serrated edges on the leading edge of owls' <a href="/wiki/Remiges" class="mw-redirect" title="Remiges">remiges</a> muffle an owl's wing beats, allowing an owl's flight to be practically silent. Some fish-eating owls, for which silence has no evolutionary advantage, lack this adaptation.
</p><p>An owl's sharp <a href="/wiki/Beak" title="Beak">beak</a> and powerful <a href="/wiki/Talon_(anatomy)" class="mw-redirect" title="Talon (anatomy)">talons</a> allow it to kill its prey before swallowing it whole (if it is not too big). Scientists studying the diets of owls are helped by their habit of <a href="/wiki/Regurgitation_(digestion)" title="Regurgitation (digestion)">regurgitating</a> the indigestible parts of their prey (such as bones, scales, and fur) in the form of <a href="/wiki/Pellet_(ornithology)" title="Pellet (ornithology)">pellets</a>. These "owl pellets" are plentiful and easy to interpret, and are often sold by companies to schools for dissection by students as a lesson in biology and ecology.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>37<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Breeding_and_reproduction">Breeding and reproduction</h3></div>
<p>Owl eggs typically have a white color and an almost spherical shape, and range in number from a few to a dozen, depending on species and the particular season; for most, three or four is the more common number. In at least one species, female owls do not mate with the same male for a lifetime. Female burrowing owls commonly travel and find other mates, while the male stays in his territory and mates with other females.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>38<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Evolution_and_systematics">Evolution and systematics</h2></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Owl_sleeping_in_tree.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Owl_sleeping_in_tree.jpg/250px-Owl_sleeping_in_tree.jpg" decoding="async" width="190" height="285" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Owl_sleeping_in_tree.jpg/500px-Owl_sleeping_in_tree.jpg 2x" data-file-width="534" data-file-height="800" /></a><figcaption>A <a href="/wiki/Great_horned_owl" title="Great horned owl">great horned owl</a> (<i>Bubo virginianus</i>) sleeping during daytime in a hollow tree</figcaption></figure>
<p>Recent <a href="/wiki/Phylogenetic" class="mw-redirect" title="Phylogenetic">phylogenetic</a> studies place owls within the <a href="/wiki/Clade" title="Clade">clade</a> <a href="/wiki/Telluraves" title="Telluraves">Telluraves</a>, most closely related to the <a href="/wiki/Accipitrimorphae" title="Accipitrimorphae">Accipitrimorphae</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Coraciimorphae" title="Coraciimorphae">Coraciimorphae</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Jarvis2014&#95;39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jarvis2014-39"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>39<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-kuhl2020&#95;40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kuhl2020-40"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>40<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> although the exact placement within Telluraves is disputed.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Prum2015&#95;41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Prum2015-41"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>41<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-HoudeBraun2019&#95;42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HoudeBraun2019-42"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>42<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>See below <a href="/wiki/Cladogram" title="Cladogram">cladogram</a>:
</p>

<td class="clade-slabel last">
</td></tr></tbody></table></div>
</td></tr>

<td class="clade-slabel last">
</td></tr></tbody></table></div>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td class="clade-slabel last">
</td></tr></tbody></table></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Cladogram" title="Cladogram">Cladogram</a> of Telluraves relationships based on Braun &amp; Kimball (2021)<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Braun&amp;Kimball2021&#95;43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Braun&amp;Kimball2021-43"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>43<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Some 220 to 225 extant species of owls are known, subdivided into two families: 1. <a href="/wiki/True_owl" class="mw-redirect" title="True owl">true owls</a> or typical owls family (<a href="/wiki/Strigidae" title="Strigidae">Strigidae</a>) and 2. barn-owls family (<a href="/wiki/Tytonidae" title="Tytonidae">Tytonidae</a>). Some entirely extinct families have also been erected based on <a href="/wiki/Fossil" title="Fossil">fossil</a> remains; these differ much from modern owls in being less specialized or specialized in a very different way (such as the terrestrial <a href="/wiki/Sophiornithidae" title="Sophiornithidae">Sophiornithidae</a>). The <a href="/wiki/Paleocene" title="Paleocene">Paleocene</a> genera <i><a href="/wiki/Berruornis" title="Berruornis">Berruornis</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Ogygoptynx" title="Ogygoptynx">Ogygoptynx</a></i> show that owls were already present as a distinct lineage some 60–57 <a href="/wiki/Million_years_ago" title="Million years ago">million years ago</a> (Mya), hence, possibly also some 5 million years earlier, at the extinction of the non-avian <a href="/wiki/Dinosaur" title="Dinosaur">dinosaurs</a>. This makes them one of the oldest known groups of non-<a href="/wiki/Galloanserae" class="mw-redirect" title="Galloanserae">Galloanserae</a> landbirds. The supposed "<a href="/wiki/Cretaceous" title="Cretaceous">Cretaceous</a> owls" <i><a href="/wiki/Bradycneme" title="Bradycneme">Bradycneme</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Heptasteornis" title="Heptasteornis">Heptasteornis</a></i> are apparently non-<a href="/wiki/Avialae" title="Avialae">avialan</a> <a href="/wiki/Maniraptora" title="Maniraptora">maniraptors</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Mortimer&#95;44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mortimer-44"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>44<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>During the <a href="/wiki/Paleogene" title="Paleogene">Paleogene</a>, the Strigiformes <a href="/wiki/Adaptive_radiation" title="Adaptive radiation">radiated</a> into <a href="/wiki/Ecological_niche" title="Ecological niche">ecological niches</a> now mostly filled by other groups of birds. <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (July 2010)">clarification needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> The owls as known today, though, evolved their characteristic <a href="/wiki/Morphology_(biology)" title="Morphology (biology)">morphology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Adaptation" title="Adaptation">adaptations</a> during that time, too. By the early <a href="/wiki/Neogene" title="Neogene">Neogene</a>, the other lineages had been displaced by other bird orders, leaving only barn owls and typical owls. The latter at that time was usually a fairly generic type of (probably earless) owl similar to today's North American <a href="/wiki/Spotted_owl" title="Spotted owl">spotted owl</a> or the European <a href="/wiki/Tawny_owl" title="Tawny owl">tawny owl</a>; the diversity in size and <a href="/wiki/Ecology" title="Ecology">ecology</a> found in typical owls today developed only subsequently.
</p><p>Around the Paleogene-Neogene boundary (some 25 Mya), barn owls were the dominant group of owls in southern Europe and adjacent Asia at least; the distribution of fossil and present-day owl lineages indicates that their decline is contemporary with the evolution of the different major lineages of true owls, which for the most part seems to have taken place in Eurasia. In the Americas, rather, an expansion of immigrant lineages of ancestral typical owls occurred.
</p><p>The supposed fossil <a href="/wiki/Heron" title="Heron">herons</a> <i>"Ardea" perplexa</i> (Middle Miocene of Sansan, France) and <i>"Ardea" lignitum</i> (Late Pliocene of Germany) were more probably owls; the latter was apparently close to the modern genus <i><a href="/wiki/Bubo_(bird)" class="mw-redirect" title="Bubo (bird)">Bubo</a></i>. Judging from this, the Late Miocene remains from France described as <i>"Ardea" aureliensis</i> should also be restudied.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Olson&#95;45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Olson-45"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>45<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Messelasturidae" title="Messelasturidae">Messelasturidae</a>, some of which were initially believed to be <a href="/wiki/Basal_(phylogenetics)" title="Basal (phylogenetics)">basal</a> Strigiformes, are now generally accepted to be diurnal <a href="/wiki/Birds_of_prey" class="mw-redirect" title="Birds of prey">birds of prey</a> showing some <a href="/wiki/Convergent_evolution" title="Convergent evolution">convergent evolution</a> toward owls. The taxa often united under <i><a href="/wiki/Strigogyps" title="Strigogyps">Strigogyps</a></i><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Mayr&#95;46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mayr-46"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>46<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> were formerly placed in part with the owls, specifically the Sophiornithidae; they appear to be <a href="/wiki/Ameghinornithidae" class="mw-redirect" title="Ameghinornithidae">Ameghinornithidae</a> instead.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Alvarenga&#95;47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Alvarenga-47"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>47<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Alvarenga2&#95;48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Alvarenga2-48"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>48<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Peters&#95;49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Peters-49"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>49<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Palaeoglaux_artophoron.JPG" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Palaeoglaux_artophoron.JPG/250px-Palaeoglaux_artophoron.JPG" decoding="async" width="190" height="242" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Palaeoglaux_artophoron.JPG/500px-Palaeoglaux_artophoron.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1292" data-file-height="1644" /></a><figcaption>The ancient fossil owl <i><a href="/wiki/Palaeoglaux" title="Palaeoglaux">Palaeoglaux</a> artophoron</i></figcaption></figure>
<p>For fossil species and <a href="/wiki/Paleosubspecies" class="mw-redirect" title="Paleosubspecies">paleosubspecies</a> of extant <a href="/wiki/Taxa" class="mw-redirect" title="Taxa">taxa</a>, see the genus and species articles. For a full list of extant and recently extinct owls, see the article <a href="/wiki/List_of_owl_species" title="List of owl species">List of owl species</a>.
</p><p><b>Unresolved and basal forms</b> (all fossil)
</p>
<ul><li><i><a href="/wiki/Berruornis" title="Berruornis">Berruornis</a></i> (Late Paleocene of France) basal? Sophornithidae?</li>
<li>Strigiformes gen. et sp. indet. (Late Paleocene of Zhylga, Kazakhstan)<sup id="cite&#95;ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>50<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Primoptynx" title="Primoptynx">Primoptynx</a></i> (Early Eocene of Wyoming, U.S.)<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Mayr2020&#95;51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mayr2020-51"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>51<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li>

Sophiornithidae?<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. <ul><li><i><a href="/wiki/Eostrix" title="Eostrix">Eostrix</a></i> (Early Eocene of United States, Europe, and Mongolia). <i>E. gulottai</i> is the smallest known fossil (or living) owl.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>53<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li>

<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Tytonidae">Tytonidae</h3></div>
<ul><li>Genus <i><a href="/wiki/Tyto" title="Tyto">Tyto</a></i> – the barn owls, grass owls, and masked owls, stand up to 500&#160;mm (20&#160;in) tall; some 15 extant species and possibly one recently extinct</li>
<li>Genus <i><a href="/wiki/Bay_owl" title="Bay owl">Phodilus</a></i> – the bay owls, two to three extant species and possibly one recently extinct</li></ul>

<li><i>Necrobyas</i> (Late Eocene/Early Oligocene – Late Miocene) includes <i>"Bubo" arvernensis</i> and <i>Paratyto</i></li>
<li><i>Prosybris</i> (Early Oligocene? – Early Miocene)</li></ul>
<p><b>Placement unresolved</b>
</p>
<ul><li>Tytonidae <i>gen. et sp. indet.</i> "TMT 164" (Middle Miocene) – <i>Prosybris</i>?</li></ul>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Strigidae">Strigidae</h3></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Asio_otus_Downsview_Park_Toronto2.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Asio_otus_Downsview_Park_Toronto2.jpg/250px-Asio_otus_Downsview_Park_Toronto2.jpg" decoding="async" width="190" height="314" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Asio_otus_Downsview_Park_Toronto2.jpg/500px-Asio_otus_Downsview_Park_Toronto2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3252" data-file-height="5379" /></a><figcaption>A <a href="/wiki/Long-eared_owl" title="Long-eared owl">long-eared owl</a> (<i>Asio otus</i>) in an erect pose</figcaption></figure>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Keulemans_Laughing_Owl.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Keulemans_Laughing_Owl.jpg/250px-Keulemans_Laughing_Owl.jpg" decoding="async" width="190" height="220" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Keulemans_Laughing_Owl.jpg/500px-Keulemans_Laughing_Owl.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1260" data-file-height="1456" /></a><figcaption>The <a href="/wiki/Laughing_owl" title="Laughing owl">laughing owl</a> (<i>Ninox albifacies</i>), last seen in 1914</figcaption></figure>
<ul><li>Genus <i><a href="/wiki/Aegolius" title="Aegolius">Aegolius</a></i> – the saw-whet owls, four species</li>
<li>Genus <i><a href="/wiki/Asio" title="Asio">Asio</a></i> – the eared owls, eight species</li>
<li>Genus <i><a href="/wiki/Athene_(bird)" title="Athene (bird)">Athene</a></i> – two to four species (depending on whether the genera <i>Speotyto</i> and <i>Heteroglaux</i> are included or not)</li>
<li>Genus <i><a href="/wiki/Horned_owl" title="Horned owl">Bubo</a></i> – the horned owls, eagle-owls and fish-owls; paraphyletic with the genera <i>Nyctea</i>, <i>Ketupa</i>, and <i>Scotopelia</i>, some 25 species</li>
<li>Genus <i><a href="/wiki/Pygmy_owl" title="Pygmy owl">Glaucidium</a></i> – the pygmy owls, about 30–35 species</li>
<li>Genus <i><a href="/wiki/Puerto_Rican_owl" title="Puerto Rican owl">Gymnasio</a></i> – the Puerto Rican owl</li>
<li>Genus <i><a href="/wiki/Bare-legged_owl" title="Bare-legged owl">Gymnoglaux</a></i> – the bare-legged owl or Cuban screech-owl</li>
<li>Genus <i><a href="/wiki/Crested_owl" title="Crested owl">Lophostrix</a></i> – the crested owl</li>
<li>Genus <i><a href="/wiki/Maned_owl" title="Maned owl">Jubula</a></i> – the maned owl</li>
<li>Genus <i><a href="/wiki/Screech_owl" title="Screech owl">Megascops</a></i> – the screech owls, some 20 species</li>
<li>Genus <i><a href="/wiki/Elf_owl" title="Elf owl">Micrathene</a></i> – the elf owl</li>
<li>Genus <i><a href="/wiki/Ninox" title="Ninox">Ninox</a></i> – the Australasian hawk-owls or boobooks, some 20 species</li>
<li>Genus <i><a href="/wiki/Scops_owl" title="Scops owl">Otus</a></i> – the scops owls; probably <a href="/wiki/Paraphyletic" class="mw-redirect" title="Paraphyletic">paraphyletic</a>, about 45 species</li>
<li>Genus <i><a href="/wiki/Jamaican_owl" title="Jamaican owl">Pseudoscops</a></i> – the Jamaican owl</li>
<li>Genus <i><a href="/wiki/Flammulated_owl" title="Flammulated owl">Psiloscops</a></i> – the flammulated owl</li>
<li>Genus <i><a href="/wiki/Ptilopsis" title="Ptilopsis">Ptilopsis</a></i> – the white-faced owls, two species</li>
<li>Genus <i><a href="/wiki/Pulsatrix" title="Pulsatrix">Pulsatrix</a></i> – the spectacled owls, three species</li>
<li>Genus <i><a href="/wiki/Strix_(genus)" class="mw-redirect" title="Strix (genus)">Strix</a></i> – the earless owls, about 15 species, including four previously assigned to <i>Ciccaba</i></li>
<li>Genus <i><a href="/wiki/Northern_hawk-owl" title="Northern hawk-owl">Surnia</a></i> – the northern hawk-owl</li>
<li>Genus <i><a href="/wiki/Taenioptynx" title="Taenioptynx">Taenioptynx</a></i> - the collared owlet</li>
<li>Genus <i><a href="/wiki/Papuan_hawk-owl" title="Papuan hawk-owl">Uroglaux</a></i> – the Papuan hawk-owl</li>
<li>Genus <i><a href="/wiki/Long-whiskered_owlet" title="Long-whiskered owlet">Xenoglaux</a></i> – the long-whiskered owlet</li></ul>
<p><b>Extinct genera</b>
</p>
<ul><li>Genus <i><a href="/wiki/Stilt-owl" title="Stilt-owl">Grallistrix</a></i> – the stilt-owls, four species; <a href="/wiki/Late_Quaternary_prehistoric_birds" class="mw-redirect" title="Late Quaternary prehistoric birds">prehistoric</a></li>
<li>Genus <i><a href="/wiki/Ornimegalonyx" title="Ornimegalonyx">Ornimegalonyx</a></i> – the Caribbean giant owls, one to two species; <a href="/wiki/Late_Quaternary_prehistoric_birds" class="mw-redirect" title="Late Quaternary prehistoric birds">prehistoric</a></li></ul>
<p><b>Fossil genera</b>
</p>
<ul><li><i>Mioglaux</i> (Late Oligocene? – Early Miocene of West-Central Europe) – includes <i>"Bubo" poirreiri</i></li>
<li><i>Intutula</i> (Early/Middle – ?Late Miocene of Central Europe) – includes <i>"Strix/Ninox" brevis</i></li>
<li><i>Alasio</i> (Middle Miocene of Vieux-Collonges, France) – includes <i>"Strix" collongensis</i></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Oraristrix" title="Oraristrix">Oraristrix</a></i> – the Brea owl (Late Pleistocene)</li></ul>
<p><b>Placement unresolved</b>
</p>
<ul><li><i>"Otus/Strix" wintershofensis</i>: <a href="/wiki/Fossil" title="Fossil">fossil</a> (Early/Middle Miocene of Wintershof West, Germany) – may be close to extant genus <i>Ninox</i><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Olson&#95;45-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Olson-45"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>45<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li>
<li><i>"Strix" edwardsi</i> – <a href="/wiki/Fossil" title="Fossil">fossil</a> (Middle/Late? Miocene)</li>
<li><i>"Asio" pygmaeus</i> – <a href="/wiki/Fossil" title="Fossil">fossil</a> (Early Pliocene of Odesa, Ukraine)</li>
<li>Strigidae gen. et sp. indet. UMMP V31030 (Late Pliocene)&#160;– <i>Strix/Bubo</i>?</li>
<li>the Ibizan owl, Strigidae <i>gen. et sp. indet.</i> – <a href="/wiki/Late_Quaternary_prehistoric_birds" class="mw-redirect" title="Late Quaternary prehistoric birds">prehistoric</a><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Sanchez&#95;54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sanchez-54"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>54<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li></ul>
<p><span class="anchor" id="symbolism&#95;and&#95;mythology"></span>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Symbolism_and_mythology">Symbolism and mythology</h2></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Africa">Africa</h3></div>
<p>Among the <a href="/wiki/Kikuyu_people" title="Kikuyu people">Kikuyu</a> of <a href="/wiki/Kenya" title="Kenya">Kenya</a>, it was believed that owls were harbingers of death. If one saw an owl or heard its hoot, someone was going to die. In general, owls are viewed as harbingers of bad luck, ill health, or death. The belief is widespread even today.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>55<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="South_Asia">South Asia</h3></div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a>, an owl is the <i><a href="/wiki/Vahana" title="Vahana">vahana</a></i> (mount) of the goddess <a href="/wiki/Lakshmi" title="Lakshmi">Lakshmi</a>, especially in the eastern region of India.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Chopra2017&#95;56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chopra2017-56"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>56<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Owls are considered a symbol of wealth, prosperity, wisdom, good luck, and fortune. This is the reason why Owls are seen with Lakshmi, who is also the goddess of fortune, wealth, and prosperity. At the same time, owls are also associated with evil times in Hinduism. At times, <a href="/wiki/Chamunda" title="Chamunda">Chamunda</a> (fearsome form of <a href="/wiki/Chandi" title="Chandi">Chandi</a>) is depicted seated on an owl, her <a href="/wiki/Vahana" title="Vahana">vahana</a> (mount or vehicle). Hindus believe that owls are messengers of death.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>57<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint noexcerpt Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Questionable_sources" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="Not only is this a blog, but the link is to a tag not to a specific post. (February 2023)">better&#160;source&#160;needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional">
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 150px; height: 150px;"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Lakshmi_02349.JPG" class="mw-file-description" title="The Hindu goddess Lakshmi with the owl"><img alt="The Hindu goddess Lakshmi with the owl" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Lakshmi_02349.JPG/120px-Lakshmi_02349.JPG" decoding="async" width="90" height="120" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Lakshmi_02349.JPG/250px-Lakshmi_02349.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1728" data-file-height="2304" /></a></span></div>
			<div class="gallerytext">The <a href="/wiki/Devi" title="Devi">Hindu goddess</a> <a href="/wiki/Lakshmi" title="Lakshmi">Lakshmi</a> with the owl</div>
		</li>
</ul>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="East_Asia">East Asia</h3></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="China">China</h4></div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>, owls are traditionally considered a sign of evil or misfortune, but are also seen as a symbol of good luck in military affairs, a symbol of protection and warding off evil spirits at funerals,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>58<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and even as the incarnation of ancestors.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>59<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Owls are one of the most common birds in carvings and paintings on bronze, pottery, and funerary objects.
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional">
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 150px; height: 150px;"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Owl%27s_face.jpg" class="mw-file-description" title="Chinese owl pottery"><img alt="Chinese owl pottery" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Owl%27s_face.jpg/120px-Owl%27s_face.jpg" decoding="async" width="120" height="90" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Owl%27s_face.jpg/250px-Owl%27s_face.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="600" /></a></span></div>
			<div class="gallerytext">Chinese owl pottery</div>
		</li>
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px">

		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px">
