			<div class="gallerytext">Chinese bronze vessel with a double-sided owl shape — 2</div>
		</li>
</ul>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Japan">Japan</h4></div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a>, owls are regarded as lucky,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>60<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> although in ancient times they were associated with death.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>61<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</h3></div>
<p>In Sumerian, Akkadian, and Babylonian culture, the owl was associated with <a href="/wiki/Lilith" title="Lilith">Lilith</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>62<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Europe">Europe</h3></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Western_culture" title="Western culture">modern West</a> generally associates owls with <a href="/wiki/Wisdom" title="Wisdom">wisdom</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alertness" title="Alertness">vigilance</a>. This link goes back at least as far as <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">Ancient Greece</a>, where <a href="/wiki/Athens" title="Athens">Athens</a>, noted for art and scholarship, and <a href="/wiki/Athena" title="Athena">Athena</a>, Athens' patron goddess and the goddess of wisdom, had <a href="/wiki/Owl_of_Athena" title="Owl of Athena">the owl</a> as a symbol.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Deacy,&#95;Susan&#95;2001&#95;63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Deacy,_Susan_2001-63"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>63<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Marija_Gimbutas" title="Marija Gimbutas">Marija Gimbutas</a> traces veneration of the owl as a goddess, among other birds, to the culture of <a href="/wiki/Old_European_culture" class="mw-redirect" title="Old European culture">Old Europe</a>, long pre-dating <a href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-Europeans" title="Proto-Indo-Europeans">Indo-European</a> cultures.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>64<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In medieval Europe, the owl was often used with more negative symbolism. The bird was associated with darkness, uncleanliness, and mourning. The bird was linked to the <a href="/wiki/Bubonic_plague" title="Bubonic plague">bubonic plague</a>. Owls were even used as anti-Semitic caricatures of Jews.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>65<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>66<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/T._F._Thiselton-Dyer" title="T. F. Thiselton-Dyer">T. F. Thiselton-Dyer</a>, in his 1883 <i>Folk-lore of Shakespeare</i>, says that </p><blockquote><p>from the earliest period it has been considered a bird of ill-omen," and <a href="/wiki/Pliny_the_Elder" title="Pliny the Elder">Pliny</a> tells us how, on one occasion, even <a href="/wiki/Rome" title="Rome">Rome</a> itself underwent a <a href="/wiki/Lustratio" title="Lustratio">lustration</a>, because one of them strayed into the Capitol. He represents it also as a funereal bird, a monster of the night, the very abomination of humankind. <a href="/wiki/Virgil" title="Virgil">Virgil</a> describes its death howl from the top of the temple by night, a circumstance introduced as a precursor of <a href="/wiki/Dido" title="Dido">Dido</a>'s death. <a href="/wiki/Ovid" title="Ovid">Ovid</a>, too, constantly speaks of this bird's presence as an evil omen; and indeed the same notions respecting it may be found among the writings of most of the ancient poets.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>67<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></p></blockquote>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Native_American_cultures">Native American cultures</h3></div>
<p>People often allude to the reputation of owls as bearers of supernatural danger when they tell misbehaving children, "the owls will get you",<sup id="cite&#95;ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>68<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and in most Native American folklore, owls are a symbol of death.
</p><p>According to the <a href="/wiki/Apache" title="Apache">Apache</a> and <a href="/wiki/Seminole" title="Seminole">Seminole</a> tribes, hearing owls hooting is considered the subject of numerous "<a href="/wiki/Bogeyman" title="Bogeyman">bogeyman</a>" stories told to warn children to remain indoors at night or not to cry too much, otherwise the owl may carry them away.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>69<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>70<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In some tribal legends, owls are associated with <a href="/wiki/Ghost" title="Ghost">spirits of the dead</a>, and the bony circles around an owl's eyes are said to comprise the fingernails of apparitional humans. Sometimes owls are said to carry messages from beyond the grave or deliver supernatural warnings to people who have broken tribal taboos.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>71<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Aztec" class="mw-redirect" title="Aztec">Aztecs</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Maya_civilization" title="Maya civilization">Maya</a>, along with other natives of <a href="/wiki/Mesoamerica" title="Mesoamerica">Mesoamerica</a>, considered the owl a symbol of death and destruction. In fact, the Aztec god of death, <a href="/wiki/Mictlantecuhtli" class="mw-redirect" title="Mictlantecuhtli">Mictlantecuhtli</a>, was often depicted with owls.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>72<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> There is an old saying in <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a> that is still in use:<sup id="cite&#95;ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>73<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <i>Cuando el tecolote canta, el indio muere</i> ("When the owl cries/sings, the Indian dies"). The <i><a href="/wiki/Popol_Vuh" title="Popol Vuh">Popol Vuh</a></i>, a Mayan religious text, describes owls as messengers of <a href="/wiki/Xibalba" title="Xibalba">Xibalba</a> (the Mayan "Place of Fright").<sup id="cite&#95;ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>74<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The belief that owls are messengers and harbingers of the dark powers is also found among the <a href="/wiki/Ho-Chunk" title="Ho-Chunk">Hočągara</a> (Winnebago) of Wisconsin.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>75<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> When in earlier days the Hočągara committed the sin of killing enemies while they were within the sanctuary of the chief's lodge, an owl appeared and spoke to them in the voice of a human, saying, "From now on, the Hočągara will have no luck." This marked the beginning of the decline of their tribe.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>76<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> An owl appeared to <a href="/wiki/Glory_of_the_Morning" title="Glory of the Morning">Glory of the Morning</a>, the only female chief of the Hočąk nation, and uttered her name. Soon after, she died.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>77<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>78<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>According to the culture of the <a href="/wiki/Hopi" title="Hopi">Hopi</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Uto-Aztecan_languages" title="Uto-Aztecan languages">Uto-Aztec</a> tribe, taboos surround owls, which are associated with sorcery and other evils.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2024)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Ojibwe" title="Ojibwe">Ojibwe</a> tribes, as well as their <a href="/wiki/Aboriginal_peoples_in_Canada" class="mw-redirect" title="Aboriginal peoples in Canada">Aboriginal Canadian</a> counterparts, used an owl as a symbol for both evil and death. In addition, they used owls as a symbol of very high status of spiritual leaders of their spirituality.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Owls&#95;in&#95;Lore&#95;and&#95;Culture&#95;79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Owls_in_Lore_and_Culture-79"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>79<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Pawnee_people" title="Pawnee people">Pawnee</a> tribes viewed owls as the symbol of protection from any danger within their realms.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Owls&#95;in&#95;Lore&#95;and&#95;Culture&#95;79-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Owls_in_Lore_and_Culture-79"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>79<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Puebloan_peoples" class="mw-redirect" title="Puebloan peoples">Puebloan peoples</a> associated owls with Skeleton Man, the god of death and the spirit of fertility.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Owls&#95;in&#95;Lore&#95;and&#95;Culture&#95;79-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Owls_in_Lore_and_Culture-79"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>79<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Yakama" title="Yakama">Yakama</a> tribes use an owl as a totem, to guide where and how forests and natural resources are useful with management.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Owls&#95;in&#95;Lore&#95;and&#95;Culture&#95;79-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Owls_in_Lore_and_Culture-79"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>79<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Rodent_control">Rodent control</h2></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Owlery_at_Morton_on_the_Hill_-_geograph.org.uk_-_532355.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Owlery_at_Morton_on_the_Hill_-_geograph.org.uk_-_532355.jpg/250px-Owlery_at_Morton_on_the_Hill_-_geograph.org.uk_-_532355.jpg" decoding="async" width="190" height="253" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/99/Owlery_at_Morton_on_the_Hill_-_geograph.org.uk_-_532355.jpg 2x" data-file-width="480" data-file-height="640" /></a><figcaption>A purpose-built owl-house or owlery at a farm near <a href="/wiki/Morton_on_the_Hill" title="Morton on the Hill">Morton on the Hill</a>, England (2006)</figcaption></figure>
<p>Encouraging natural predators to control rodent population is a natural form of pest control, along with excluding food sources for rodents. Placing a <a href="/wiki/Nest_box" title="Nest box">nest box</a> for owls on a property can help control rodent populations (one family of hungry barn owls can consume more than 3,000 rodents in a nesting season) while maintaining the naturally balanced food chain.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>80<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Conservation_issues">Conservation issues</h2></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Strigiformes_by_population" title="List of Strigiformes by population">List of Strigiformes by population</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-left" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Bubo_scandiacus_(Linnaeus,_1758)_Male.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Bubo_scandiacus_%28Linnaeus%2C_1758%29_Male.jpg/250px-Bubo_scandiacus_%28Linnaeus%2C_1758%29_Male.jpg" decoding="async" width="190" height="230" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Bubo_scandiacus_%28Linnaeus%2C_1758%29_Male.jpg/500px-Bubo_scandiacus_%28Linnaeus%2C_1758%29_Male.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2658" data-file-height="3222" /></a><figcaption>The <a href="/wiki/Snowy_owl" title="Snowy owl">snowy owl</a> is very <a href="/wiki/Endangered" class="mw-redirect" title="Endangered">endangered</a> in <a href="/wiki/Scandinavia" title="Scandinavia">Scandinavia</a><sup id="cite&#95;ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>81<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a>, where it is found only in northern <a href="/wiki/Lapland_(Finland)" title="Lapland (Finland)">Lapland</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>82<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></figcaption></figure>
<p>Almost all owls are listed in Appendix II of the international <a href="/wiki/CITES" title="CITES">CITES</a> treaty (the Convention on Illegal Trade in Endangered Species of Wild Fauna and Flora) with four species listed in Appendix I. Although owls have long been hunted, a 2008 news story from Malaysia indicates that the magnitude of owl <a href="/wiki/Poaching" title="Poaching">poaching</a> may be on the rise. In November 2008, <a href="/wiki/Traffic_(conservation_programme)" title="Traffic (conservation programme)">TRAFFIC</a> reported the seizure of 900 plucked and "oven-ready" owls in Peninsular <a href="/wiki/Malaysia" title="Malaysia">Malaysia</a>. Said Chris Shepherd, Senior Programme Officer for TRAFFIC's Southeast Asia office, "This is the first time we know of where 'ready-prepared' owls have been seized in Malaysia, and it may mark the start of a new trend in wild meat from the region. We will be monitoring developments closely." TRAFFIC commended the Department of Wildlife and National Parks in Malaysia for the raid that exposed the huge haul of owls. Included in the seizure were dead and plucked barn owls, spotted wood owls, crested serpent eagles, barred eagles, and brown wood owls, as well as 7,000 live lizards.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>83<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In addition to hunting, other threats to owl populations are <a href="/wiki/Habitat_loss" class="mw-redirect" title="Habitat loss">habitat loss</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pesticides" class="mw-redirect" title="Pesticides">pesticides</a>, <a href="/wiki/Viruses" class="mw-redirect" title="Viruses">viruses</a>, and vehicle collisions.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>84<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>85<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div style="clear:both;" class=""></div>
